احکام نظریہ کون الاجماع حجة وغيرہ کو خارج کرنا مقصود ہے اور وہ الا حکام کی تعریف میں جو لاقتضاء او تحیر کی قید ہے اس سے خارج ہو رہے ہیں کیونکہ کون الا جماع حجتہ وغیرہ میں
The MSK al-Malih, Al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih, Sharh al-Tawdih. This statement of the commentator Taftazani that soundness and corruption are among rational rulings is wrong because the conformity or opposition of an action to the law occurs only after the advent of revelation and depends on the law, so it is a legal ruling, not rational. Also, the definition of soundness and corruption that the commentator mentioned is consistent with the school of the theologians, not with the school of the jurists, whereas here the meaning according to the jurists should have been stated. According to the jurists, the definition of soundness of an act is: it being such that it removes liability (qada), and corruption is the opposite; this is undoubtedly a legal matter. Because being a remover of qada depends on the law. And the meaning of 'the prayer being recommended (mandub)' – the commentator is raising a fourth objection that the author's saying that the prayer being recommended is a ruling related to the act of a child is incorrect, because the meaning of the child's prayer being recommended is that the guardian has a command from the Lawgiver to encourage and urge the child to pray, to command him to pray, as in the hadith 'Command them to pray when they are seven years old.' So this ruling is related to the guardian, not to the child. The response: Like previous objections, this objection of the commentator is weak and flimsy because the meaning of the child's prayer being recommended is clear: he is rewarded for it, i.e., he deserves reward. That is why 'Ali was proud of his Islam at the time of being a child and said, 'I preceded you all to Islam,' although no one had urged him to Islam. Also, a woman asked the Prophet about her child: 'Is there Hajj for this one?' He said: 'Yes, and for you there is reward.' This also proves that a child gets reward for supererogatory worship. So this ruling is related to the act of the child. As for the guardian's encouragement and urging, that is another ruling. Also, what does the commentator Taftazani say about a child who has no guardian and no one has encouraged him to pray, but he simply prays by watching others? The ruling of mandub is only related to the act of the child; the guardian's encouragement does not exist at all. What is your opinion about this? Summary: All these objections of the commentator Taftazani are coercive and regrettable. So reflect. Third: The definition does not encompass—explaining the third objection, the commentator Taftazani says that the third objection is that the definition of hukm is not comprehensive and does not apply to a ruling established by qiyas, because the definition includes the condition 'speech of Allah', and a ruling from qiyas is not established by the speech of Allah but by qiyas. Response: The response given to this is that a ruling established by qiyas is in fact established by the speech of Allah, but it is hidden; qiyas only manifests it. So it is established that in qiyas there is also the speech of Allah, and the ruling is established by it; qiyas is only a revealer, not an establisher. And it is not hidden from you that the questioner is objecting to the author: to specify the third objection with what is established by qiyas is not correct, because this problem applies not only to what is established by qiyas but also to what is established by sunnah and what is established by ijma. Because a ruling established by sunnah is not established by the speech of Allah but by the speech of the Messenger, and likewise a ruling established by ijma is not established by the speech of Allah. Hence, specification with what is established by qiyas is not correct. Response: The response to this is that here too, in reality, the ruling is established by the speech of Allah; sunnah and ijma are only revealers and identifiers of this ruling and the speech, and they indicate that here too the speech exists. And the meaning of sunnah and ijma being evidences for rulings is that they are revealers and identifiers of rulings. Fourth: It is not inclusive—explanation of the fourth objection: the definition of hukm is not comprehensive; it does not apply to 'Believe' (amanu) and 'So take heed' (fa'tabiru) because the definition mentions the actions of the responsible persons (af'al al-mukallafin), and by actions, apparently outward actions (af'al al-jawarih) are meant, i.e., those performed by the outer limbs. Hence, speech related to actions of the heart (af'al al-qalb) would be excluded from the definition, such as the obligation of faith (wujub al-iman) meaning belief, and the obligation of consideration (wujub al-i'tibar) meaning qiyas. These are actions of the heart, not actions of the limbs, so the definition of hukm would not apply to them and would not be comprehensive. Fifth: Since he took—explanation of the fifth objection: that in the definition of fiqh, the restriction 'al-`amaliyyah' (practical) is redundant because when the definition of hukm includes the restriction 'actions of the responsible persons' and by actions, outward actions are meant, then actions of the responsible persons become specific to outward acts (al-`amalat), and theoretical rulings (ahkam nazariyyah) are excluded from it. Hence, after that, the restriction 'al-`amaliyyah' in the definition of fiqh is superfluous and unnecessary, because its purpose was also to exclude theoretical matters, and they are already excluded by 'actions of the responsible persons'. And he answered both—the commentator Taftazani transmits the same answer given by the author: that 'actions of the responsible persons' is general, whether they are actions of the limbs or actions of the heart, so the definition of hukm includes both and becomes comprehensive. Thus the fourth objection falls. And in the definition of fiqh, the restriction 'al-`amaliyyah' is meant only for actions of the limbs and outward acts; thereby theoretical rulings are excluded, such as the obligation of faith, the obligation of consideration, etc. Hence, mentioning 'al-`amaliyyah' is not redundant, and thus the fifth objection is also removed. And someone may say: 'When the ruling is made...' This is an objection against the author that even if 'actions of the responsible persons' is interpreted generally, the restriction 'al-`amaliyyah' still entails redundancy. This restriction is redundant in any case because by it two things were intended to be excluded: (1) the obligation of faith and the like, and (2) the fact that ijma is a proof. And both of these are already excluded before imposing the restriction 'al-`amaliyyah'. The obligation of faith etc. are excluded by the restriction 'al-shar`iyyah' because it was previously mentioned that if in the definition of fiqh, 'hukm' means hukm in the technical sense, then 'al-shar`iyyah' means 'what depends on the law'. When 'al-shar`iyyah' means 'what depends on the law', then the obligation of faith and the like are excluded from it because they are not dependent on the law. (2) Second, by this, theoretical rulings such as that ijma is a proof, etc., are intended to be excluded, and they are excluded by the restriction in the definition of hukm that is 'requirement or option' (iqtida' or takhyir), because in the fact that ijma is a proof, etc.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) on Taftazani's commentary on Al-Tawdih. The author first corrects Taftazani's error regarding the nature of legal soundness and corruption, arguing they are legal, not rational. He then addresses four objections to the definitions of hukm and fiqh. The first objection (misnumbered as fourth in the text) claims that the mandub status of a child's prayer relates to the guardian, not the child; the author refutes this by citing the hadith 'Command them to pray at seven' and examples of reward for children's acts. The second objection (third) argues that the definition of hukm excludes rulings from qiyas, sunnah, and ijma since they are not directly from Allah's speech; the response is that these sources merely reveal the divine ruling, which is already present in Allah's speech. The third objection (fourth) claims the definition excludes actions of the heart (e.g., faith, reasoning); the response is that 'actions of the responsible persons' includes heart actions. The fourth objection (fifth) claims the term 'amaliyyah in the definition of fiqh is redundant; the response is that 'amaliyyah specifically limits fiqh to outward actions, while theoretical matters like the obligation of faith are kept distinct. An additional objection is raised that even with generalization, redundancy remains, and the author begins to address it by showing how certain theoretical matters are excluded by other parts of the definition. The passage demonstrates the Hanafi usul method of defending definitions against objections using textual proofs and rational analysis.

Key Terms
حكم (ḥukm): Speech of Allah related to the actions of the responsible persons by way of requirement, option, or consideration.
صحة (ṣaḥāḥ (sihhah)): Soundness: the act removes liability (qaḍā'); a legal attribute, not rational.
فساد (fasād): Corruption: the opposite of soundness; the act does not remove liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is Taftazani's view that sihhah and fasad are rational rulings incorrect?
A) Because rational rulings are only about good and bad.
B) Because these attributes appear only after revelation and are thus legal.
C) Because the fuqaha define them differently.
D) Because the Mutakallimun agree with the Hanafis.

Key Takeaway
Sihhah and fasad are legal rulings, not rational, because they depend on the advent of revelation.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
8 min
The Mandub Status of a Child's Act
Refutation of Taftazani's objection regarding child's prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the child's prayer is mandub and how the ruling relates to the child, not the guardian.

Core Idea
A child's act of worship is mandub and rewarded, even if no guardian commands it.

Taftazani objects that the mandub status of a child's prayer is actually a command to the guardian, not a ruling on the child's act. The author refutes this by citing evidence that the child receives reward directly, proving the ruling is on the child's act. This demonstrates how the Hanafi school understands the scope of legal address.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Taftazani claims that when the author says 'the prayer is mandub', the ruling applies to the guardian, not the child, because the hadith commands the guardian to urge the child.
He uses the hadith 'Command them to pray at seven'.
2
Identify the Hanafi response
The author responds that the mandub status of the child's act is clear: the child is rewarded for it. This is independent of the guardian's command.
3
Examine the evidence
The author cites 'Ali's pride in his early Islam and the hadith of the woman asking about her child's Hajj, where the Prophet affirmed reward for both child and mother.
These show that children get reward for supererogatory acts.
4
Apply to an edge case
What about a child with no guardian who prays by imitation? The ruling of mandub still applies to the child's act.
This proves the ruling is on the child's act, not on the guardian.

ہیں نہ کہ افعال جوارح لہذا تعریف حکم ان پر صادق نہیں آئے گی اور جامع نہیں رہے گی۔ الخامس انه لما اخذ الخ پانچویں اشکال کی تشریح ہے کہ فقہ کی تعریف میں العملیہ کی قید میں تکرار ہے کیونکہ جب حکم کی تعریف میں افعال مکلفین کی قید لگائی گئی اور افعال سے افعال جوارح مراد لیے گئے تو افعال مکلفین خاص ہونگے اعمال (عملیات) کے ساتھ اور احکام نظریہ اس سے خارج ہو جا ئینگے لہذا اس کے بعد فقہ کی تعریف میں العملیہ کی قید مستدرک اور بے کار ہوگی کیونکہ اس سے بھی مقصود نظریات کو خارج کرنا تھا اور وہ افعال مکلفین سے ہی خارج ہو گئے ہیں۔ واجاب عنهما شارح تفتازانى چوتھے اور پانچویں اشکال کا وہی جواب نقل کر رہے سے مراد فقط افعال جوارح اور عملیات ہیں اس سے احکام نظریہ خارج ہو جائیں گے مثلا وجوب ایمان وجوب اعتبار وغیرہ لہذا العملیہ کا ذکر تکرار نہیں ہوگا تو یا نچواں اشکال بھی رفع ہو گیا ولقائل أن يقول اذا جعل الحكم الخ یہ اشکال علی المصنف ہے کہ اگر افعال مکلفین میں تقسیم کی جائے تب بھی العملیہ کی قید میں تکرار لازم آتا ہے یہ قید ہر حال میں مکرر ہے کیونکہ العملیہ کی قید سے دو چیزوں کو خارج کرنا مقصود تھا (۱) وجوب ایمان و غیره (۲) كون الاجماع حجۃ اور یہ دونوں العملیہ کی قید لگانے سے پہلے خارج ہو چکے ہیں وجوب ایمان وغیرہ الشرعیہ کی قید سے خارج ہوئے ہیں کیونکہ پہلے گزر چکا ہے کہ اگر فقہ کی تعریف میں حکم سے حکم اصطلاحی مراد ہو تو الشرعیہ کا معنى ما يتوقف على الشرع ہوگا جب الشرعیہ بمعنی ما توقف علی الشرع ہے تو وجوب ایمان وغیرہ اس سے خارج ہو جائیں گے کیونکہ وہ ما توقف علی الشرع نہیں ہیں (۲) دوسرا اس سے احکام نظریہ کون الاجماع حجة وغيرہ کو خارج کرنا مقصود ہے اور وہ الا حکام کی تعریف میں جو لاقتضاء او تحیر کی قید ہے اس سے خارج ہو رہے ہیں کیونکہ کون الا جماع حجتہ وغیرہ میں
The MSK al-Malih, Al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih, Sharh al-Tawdih. This statement of the commentator Taftazani that soundness and corruption are among rational rulings is wrong because the conformity or opposition of an action to the law occurs only after the advent of revelation and depends on the law, so it is a legal ruling, not rational. Also, the definition of soundness and corruption that the commentator mentioned is consistent with the school of the theologians, not with the school of the jurists, whereas here the meaning according to the jurists should have been stated. According to the jurists, the definition of soundness of an act is: it being such that it removes liability (qada), and corruption is the opposite; this is undoubtedly a legal matter. Because being a remover of qada depends on the law. And the meaning of 'the prayer being recommended (mandub)' – the commentator is raising a fourth objection that the author's saying that the prayer being recommended is a ruling related to the act of a child is incorrect, because the meaning of the child's prayer being recommended is that the guardian has a command from the Lawgiver to encourage and urge the child to pray, to command him to pray, as in the hadith 'Command them to pray when they are seven years old.' So this ruling is related to the guardian, not to the child. The response: Like previous objections, this objection of the commentator is weak and flimsy because the meaning of the child's prayer being recommended is clear: he is rewarded for it, i.e., he deserves reward. That is why 'Ali was proud of his Islam at the time of being a child and said, 'I preceded you all to Islam,' although no one had urged him to Islam. Also, a woman asked the Prophet about her child: 'Is there Hajj for this one?' He said: 'Yes, and for you there is reward.' This also proves that a child gets reward for supererogatory worship. So this ruling is related to the act of the child. As for the guardian's encouragement and urging, that is another ruling. Also, what does the commentator Taftazani say about a child who has no guardian and no one has encouraged him to pray, but he simply prays by watching others? The ruling of mandub is only related to the act of the child; the guardian's encouragement does not exist at all. What is your opinion about this? Summary: All these objections of the commentator Taftazani are coercive and regrettable. So reflect. Third: The definition does not encompass—explaining the third objection, the commentator Taftazani says that the third objection is that the definition of hukm is not comprehensive and does not apply to a ruling established by qiyas, because the definition includes the condition 'speech of Allah', and a ruling from qiyas is not established by the speech of Allah but by qiyas. Response: The response given to this is that a ruling established by qiyas is in fact established by the speech of Allah, but it is hidden; qiyas only manifests it. So it is established that in qiyas there is also the speech of Allah, and the ruling is established by it; qiyas is only a revealer, not an establisher. And it is not hidden from you that the questioner is objecting to the author: to specify the third objection with what is established by qiyas is not correct, because this problem applies not only to what is established by qiyas but also to what is established by sunnah and what is established by ijma. Because a ruling established by sunnah is not established by the speech of Allah but by the speech of the Messenger, and likewise a ruling established by ijma is not established by the speech of Allah. Hence, specification with what is established by qiyas is not correct. Response: The response to this is that here too, in reality, the ruling is established by the speech of Allah; sunnah and ijma are only revealers and identifiers of this ruling and the speech, and they indicate that here too the speech exists. And the meaning of sunnah and ijma being evidences for rulings is that they are revealers and identifiers of rulings. Fourth: It is not inclusive—explanation of the fourth objection: the definition of hukm is not comprehensive; it does not apply to 'Believe' (amanu) and 'So take heed' (fa'tabiru) because the definition mentions the actions of the responsible persons (af'al al-mukallafin), and by actions, apparently outward actions (af'al al-jawarih) are meant, i.e., those performed by the outer limbs. Hence, speech related to actions of the heart (af'al al-qalb) would be excluded from the definition, such as the obligation of faith (wujub al-iman) meaning belief, and the obligation of consideration (wujub al-i'tibar) meaning qiyas. These are actions of the heart, not actions of the limbs, so the definition of hukm would not apply to them and would not be comprehensive. Fifth: Since he took—explanation of the fifth objection: that in the definition of fiqh, the restriction 'al-`amaliyyah' (practical) is redundant because when the definition of hukm includes the restriction 'actions of the responsible persons' and by actions, outward actions are meant, then actions of the responsible persons become specific to outward acts (al-`amalat), and theoretical rulings (ahkam nazariyyah) are excluded from it. Hence, after that, the restriction 'al-`amaliyyah' in the definition of fiqh is superfluous and unnecessary, because its purpose was also to exclude theoretical matters, and they are already excluded by 'actions of the responsible persons'. And he answered both—the commentator Taftazani transmits the same answer given by the author: that 'actions of the responsible persons' is general, whether they are actions of the limbs or actions of the heart, so the definition of hukm includes both and becomes comprehensive. Thus the fourth objection falls. And in the definition of fiqh, the restriction 'al-`amaliyyah' is meant only for actions of the limbs and outward acts; thereby theoretical rulings are excluded, such as the obligation of faith, the obligation of consideration, etc. Hence, mentioning 'al-`amaliyyah' is not redundant, and thus the fifth objection is also removed. And someone may say: 'When the ruling is made...' This is an objection against the author that even if 'actions of the responsible persons' is interpreted generally, the restriction 'al-`amaliyyah' still entails redundancy. This restriction is redundant in any case because by it two things were intended to be excluded: (1) the obligation of faith and the like, and (2) the fact that ijma is a proof. And both of these are already excluded before imposing the restriction 'al-`amaliyyah'. The obligation of faith etc. are excluded by the restriction 'al-shar`iyyah' because it was previously mentioned that if in the definition of fiqh, 'hukm' means hukm in the technical sense, then 'al-shar`iyyah' means 'what depends on the law'. When 'al-shar`iyyah' means 'what depends on the law', then the obligation of faith and the like are excluded from it because they are not dependent on the law. (2) Second, by this, theoretical rulings such as that ijma is a proof, etc., are intended to be excluded, and they are excluded by the restriction in the definition of hukm that is 'requirement or option' (iqtida' or takhyir), because in the fact that ijma is a proof, etc.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) on Taftazani's commentary on Al-Tawdih. The author first corrects Taftazani's error regarding the nature of legal soundness and corruption, arguing they are legal, not rational. He then addresses four objections to the definitions of hukm and fiqh. The first objection (misnumbered as fourth in the text) claims that the mandub status of a child's prayer relates to the guardian, not the child; the author refutes this by citing the hadith 'Command them to pray at seven' and examples of reward for children's acts. The second objection (third) argues that the definition of hukm excludes rulings from qiyas, sunnah, and ijma since they are not directly from Allah's speech; the response is that these sources merely reveal the divine ruling, which is already present in Allah's speech. The third objection (fourth) claims the definition excludes actions of the heart (e.g., faith, reasoning); the response is that 'actions of the responsible persons' includes heart actions. The fourth objection (fifth) claims the term 'amaliyyah in the definition of fiqh is redundant; the response is that 'amaliyyah specifically limits fiqh to outward actions, while theoretical matters like the obligation of faith are kept distinct. An additional objection is raised that even with generalization, redundancy remains, and the author begins to address it by showing how certain theoretical matters are excluded by other parts of the definition. The passage demonstrates the Hanafi usul method of defending definitions against objections using textual proofs and rational analysis.

Key Terms
مندوب (mandūb): An act whose performance is recommended and rewarded but not obligatory; the child's act is mandub because reward is given.
ولى (walī): Guardian responsible for the child's upbringing; the passage distinguishes between the ruling on the child's act and the guardian's independent duty.
ثواب (thawāb): Reward; the passage uses reward as evidence that the act is mandub for the child.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence the author uses to prove that the child's act itself carries the mandub ruling?
A) The hadith 'Command them to pray at seven'.
B) The fact that 'Ali was proud of his early Islam and a hadith about a child's Hajj.
C) The consensus of scholars.
D) The definition of mandub.

Key Takeaway
A child's act of worship is mandub and rewarded, independent of the guardian's command.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
10 min
Hukm Definition and Rulings from Qiyas, Sunnah, Ijma
Response to the objection that hukm definition excludes qiyas, sunnah, ijma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi school resolves the tension between the definition of hukm as 'speech of Allah' and rulings derived from qiyas, sunnah, and ijma.

Core Idea
Qiyas, sunnah, and ijma reveal the ruling already present in Allah's speech; they do not establish new rulings independent of it.

A major objection to the definition of hukm is that it excludes rulings from qiyas, sunnah, and ijma, since these are not directly from Allah's speech. The author clarifies that these sources are merely revealers (kashif/muzhir) of the ruling that exists in Allah's speech. This preserves the centrality of divine address while accommodating the role of secondary sources.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
The definition of hukm requires 'speech of Allah', but rulings from qiyas, sunnah, and ijma are not directly from Allah's speech. So the definition is not comprehensive.
The objection is raised by the questioner (sa'il) criticizing the author's specification of qiyas.
2
Identify the Hanafi response
The response: In reality, the ruling is established by Allah's speech; qiyas, sunnah, and ijma only reveal it. They are kashif and mu'arraf, not independent establishers.
3
Understand the expansion
The objection is broadened: It applies not only to qiyas but also to sunnah and ijma. The author notes that the same response works for all: they all reveal the divine ruling.
This shows the unity of the usul principle.
4
Apply the principle
When we say 'the ruling is from sunnah' or 'from ijma', we mean that these sources inform us of the divine ruling, not that they create it.

احکام نظریہ کون الاجماع حجة وغيرہ کو خارج کرنا مقصود ہے اور وہ الا حکام کی تعریف میں جو لاقتضاء او تحیر کی قید ہے اس سے خارج ہو رہے ہیں کیونکہ کون الا جماع حجتہ وغیرہ میں
The MSK al-Malih, Al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih, Sharh al-Tawdih. This statement of the commentator Taftazani that soundness and corruption are among rational rulings is wrong because the conformity or opposition of an action to the law occurs only after the advent of revelation and depends on the law, so it is a legal ruling, not rational. Also, the definition of soundness and corruption that the commentator mentioned is consistent with the school of the theologians, not with the school of the jurists, whereas here the meaning according to the jurists should have been stated. According to the jurists, the definition of soundness of an act is: it being such that it removes liability (qada), and corruption is the opposite; this is undoubtedly a legal matter. Because being a remover of qada depends on the law. And the meaning of 'the prayer being recommended (mandub)' – the commentator is raising a fourth objection that the author's saying that the prayer being recommended is a ruling related to the act of a child is incorrect, because the meaning of the child's prayer being recommended is that the guardian has a command from the Lawgiver to encourage and urge the child to pray, to command him to pray, as in the hadith 'Command them to pray when they are seven years old.' So this ruling is related to the guardian, not to the child. The response: Like previous objections, this objection of the commentator is weak and flimsy because the meaning of the child's prayer being recommended is clear: he is rewarded for it, i.e., he deserves reward. That is why 'Ali was proud of his Islam at the time of being a child and said, 'I preceded you all to Islam,' although no one had urged him to Islam. Also, a woman asked the Prophet about her child: 'Is there Hajj for this one?' He said: 'Yes, and for you there is reward.' This also proves that a child gets reward for supererogatory worship. So this ruling is related to the act of the child. As for the guardian's encouragement and urging, that is another ruling. Also, what does the commentator Taftazani say about a child who has no guardian and no one has encouraged him to pray, but he simply prays by watching others? The ruling of mandub is only related to the act of the child; the guardian's encouragement does not exist at all. What is your opinion about this? Summary: All these objections of the commentator Taftazani are coercive and regrettable. So reflect. Third: The definition does not encompass—explaining the third objection, the commentator Taftazani says that the third objection is that the definition of hukm is not comprehensive and does not apply to a ruling established by qiyas, because the definition includes the condition 'speech of Allah', and a ruling from qiyas is not established by the speech of Allah but by qiyas. Response: The response given to this is that a ruling established by qiyas is in fact established by the speech of Allah, but it is hidden; qiyas only manifests it. So it is established that in qiyas there is also the speech of Allah, and the ruling is established by it; qiyas is only a revealer, not an establisher. And it is not hidden from you that the questioner is objecting to the author: to specify the third objection with what is established by qiyas is not correct, because this problem applies not only to what is established by qiyas but also to what is established by sunnah and what is established by ijma. Because a ruling established by sunnah is not established by the speech of Allah but by the speech of the Messenger, and likewise a ruling established by ijma is not established by the speech of Allah. Hence, specification with what is established by qiyas is not correct. Response: The response to this is that here too, in reality, the ruling is established by the speech of Allah; sunnah and ijma are only revealers and identifiers of this ruling and the speech, and they indicate that here too the speech exists. And the meaning of sunnah and ijma being evidences for rulings is that they are revealers and identifiers of rulings. Fourth: It is not inclusive—explanation of the fourth objection: the definition of hukm is not comprehensive; it does not apply to 'Believe' (amanu) and 'So take heed' (fa'tabiru) because the definition mentions the actions of the responsible persons (af'al al-mukallafin), and by actions, apparently outward actions (af'al al-jawarih) are meant, i.e., those performed by the outer limbs. Hence, speech related to actions of the heart (af'al al-qalb) would be excluded from the definition, such as the obligation of faith (wujub al-iman) meaning belief, and the obligation of consideration (wujub al-i'tibar) meaning qiyas. These are actions of the heart, not actions of the limbs, so the definition of hukm would not apply to them and would not be comprehensive. Fifth: Since he took—explanation of the fifth objection: that in the definition of fiqh, the restriction 'al-`amaliyyah' (practical) is redundant because when the definition of hukm includes the restriction 'actions of the responsible persons' and by actions, outward actions are meant, then actions of the responsible persons become specific to outward acts (al-`amalat), and theoretical rulings (ahkam nazariyyah) are excluded from it. Hence, after that, the restriction 'al-`amaliyyah' in the definition of fiqh is superfluous and unnecessary, because its purpose was also to exclude theoretical matters, and they are already excluded by 'actions of the responsible persons'. And he answered both—the commentator Taftazani transmits the same answer given by the author: that 'actions of the responsible persons' is general, whether they are actions of the limbs or actions of the heart, so the definition of hukm includes both and becomes comprehensive. Thus the fourth objection falls. And in the definition of fiqh, the restriction 'al-`amaliyyah' is meant only for actions of the limbs and outward acts; thereby theoretical rulings are excluded, such as the obligation of faith, the obligation of consideration, etc. Hence, mentioning 'al-`amaliyyah' is not redundant, and thus the fifth objection is also removed. And someone may say: 'When the ruling is made...' This is an objection against the author that even if 'actions of the responsible persons' is interpreted generally, the restriction 'al-`amaliyyah' still entails redundancy. This restriction is redundant in any case because by it two things were intended to be excluded: (1) the obligation of faith and the like, and (2) the fact that ijma is a proof. And both of these are already excluded before imposing the restriction 'al-`amaliyyah'. The obligation of faith etc. are excluded by the restriction 'al-shar`iyyah' because it was previously mentioned that if in the definition of fiqh, 'hukm' means hukm in the technical sense, then 'al-shar`iyyah' means 'what depends on the law'. When 'al-shar`iyyah' means 'what depends on the law', then the obligation of faith and the like are excluded from it because they are not dependent on the law. (2) Second, by this, theoretical rulings such as that ijma is a proof, etc., are intended to be excluded, and they are excluded by the restriction in the definition of hukm that is 'requirement or option' (iqtida' or takhyir), because in the fact that ijma is a proof, etc.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) on Taftazani's commentary on Al-Tawdih. The author first corrects Taftazani's error regarding the nature of legal soundness and corruption, arguing they are legal, not rational. He then addresses four objections to the definitions of hukm and fiqh. The first objection (misnumbered as fourth in the text) claims that the mandub status of a child's prayer relates to the guardian, not the child; the author refutes this by citing the hadith 'Command them to pray at seven' and examples of reward for children's acts. The second objection (third) argues that the definition of hukm excludes rulings from qiyas, sunnah, and ijma since they are not directly from Allah's speech; the response is that these sources merely reveal the divine ruling, which is already present in Allah's speech. The third objection (fourth) claims the definition excludes actions of the heart (e.g., faith, reasoning); the response is that 'actions of the responsible persons' includes heart actions. The fourth objection (fifth) claims the term 'amaliyyah in the definition of fiqh is redundant; the response is that 'amaliyyah specifically limits fiqh to outward actions, while theoretical matters like the obligation of faith are kept distinct. An additional objection is raised that even with generalization, redundancy remains, and the author begins to address it by showing how certain theoretical matters are excluded by other parts of the definition. The passage demonstrates the Hanafi usul method of defending definitions against objections using textual proofs and rational analysis.

Key Terms
خطاب اللہ (khiṭāb Allāh): Speech of Allah that addresses the actions of mukallafun; the central element in the definition of hukm.
کاشف (kāshif): Revealer; something that uncovers a pre-existing ruling. Applied to qiyas, sunnah, ijma.
مظہر (muẓhir): Manifestor; similar to kashif. Qiyas is a muẓhir of the ruling in Allah's speech.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in this passage, what is the role of qiyas in establishing a legal ruling?
A) Qiyas creates a new ruling independent of divine speech.
B) Qiyas reveals a hidden ruling that is already present in Allah's speech.
C) Qiyas only applies to matters not covered by nusus.
D) Qiyas is not a valid source of law.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas, sunnah, and ijma are revealers of the ruling already present in Allah's speech, not independent sources.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
12 min
Actions of the Heart and the Redundancy of 'Amaliyyah
Responses to fourth and fifth objections on definitions of hukm and fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the definition of hukm includes actions of the heart and why the term 'amaliyyah in the definition of fiqh is not redundant.

Core Idea
Actions of the heart are included in 'actions of the responsible persons', so 'amaliyyah restricts fiqh to limb actions, removing redundancy.

Two final objections are addressed: (4) the definition of hukm excludes actions of the heart like faith and intellectual reasoning; (5) the term 'amaliyyah in the definition of fiqh is redundant because 'actions of the responsible persons' already excludes theoretical matters. The author responds by clarifying that 'actions' includes heart actions, and 'amaliyyah further limits fiqh to practical limb actions. An additional objection argues that even with this, redundancy remains, but the text shows that theoretical matters like 'obligation of faith' are excluded by other parts of the definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fourth objection
Objection: The definition of hukm mentions 'actions of the responsible persons' which seems to refer only to outward limb actions, thus excluding heart actions like faith.
Examples: wujub al-iman (obligation of faith), wujub al-i'tibar (obligation of reasoning).
2
Identify the fifth objection
Objection: In the definition of fiqh, the term 'amaliyyah (practical) is redundant because 'actions of the responsible persons' already excludes theoretical rulings.
Theoretical rulings like 'ijma is a proof' are not actions of the limbs.
3
Understand the author's response
Response to both: 'Actions of the responsible persons' is general and includes both limb and heart actions. Therefore, the fourth objection falls. For fiqh, 'amaliyyah specifies only limb actions, excluding theoretical rulings. Hence, no redundancy.
The author uses the same answer for both objections.
4
Analyze the additional objection
A further objection: Even with general interpretation, 'amaliyyah is still redundant because theoretical rulings like 'obligation of faith' are already excluded by the condition 'shar'iyyah' (dependent on law) and the condition 'iqtida' or takhyir' in the definition of hukm.
This objection is left pending; the passage begins addressing it.

احکام نظریہ کون الاجماع حجة وغيرہ کو خارج کرنا مقصود ہے اور وہ الا حکام کی تعریف میں جو لاقتضاء او تحیر کی قید ہے اس سے خارج ہو رہے ہیں کیونکہ کون الا جماع حجتہ وغیرہ میں
The MSK al-Malih, Al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih, Sharh al-Tawdih. This statement of the commentator Taftazani that soundness and corruption are among rational rulings is wrong because the conformity or opposition of an action to the law occurs only after the advent of revelation and depends on the law, so it is a legal ruling, not rational. Also, the definition of soundness and corruption that the commentator mentioned is consistent with the school of the theologians, not with the school of the jurists, whereas here the meaning according to the jurists should have been stated. According to the jurists, the definition of soundness of an act is: it being such that it removes liability (qada), and corruption is the opposite; this is undoubtedly a legal matter. Because being a remover of qada depends on the law. And the meaning of 'the prayer being recommended (mandub)' – the commentator is raising a fourth objection that the author's saying that the prayer being recommended is a ruling related to the act of a child is incorrect, because the meaning of the child's prayer being recommended is that the guardian has a command from the Lawgiver to encourage and urge the child to pray, to command him to pray, as in the hadith 'Command them to pray when they are seven years old.' So this ruling is related to the guardian, not to the child. The response: Like previous objections, this objection of the commentator is weak and flimsy because the meaning of the child's prayer being recommended is clear: he is rewarded for it, i.e., he deserves reward. That is why 'Ali was proud of his Islam at the time of being a child and said, 'I preceded you all to Islam,' although no one had urged him to Islam. Also, a woman asked the Prophet about her child: 'Is there Hajj for this one?' He said: 'Yes, and for you there is reward.' This also proves that a child gets reward for supererogatory worship. So this ruling is related to the act of the child. As for the guardian's encouragement and urging, that is another ruling. Also, what does the commentator Taftazani say about a child who has no guardian and no one has encouraged him to pray, but he simply prays by watching others? The ruling of mandub is only related to the act of the child; the guardian's encouragement does not exist at all. What is your opinion about this? Summary: All these objections of the commentator Taftazani are coercive and regrettable. So reflect. Third: The definition does not encompass—explaining the third objection, the commentator Taftazani says that the third objection is that the definition of hukm is not comprehensive and does not apply to a ruling established by qiyas, because the definition includes the condition 'speech of Allah', and a ruling from qiyas is not established by the speech of Allah but by qiyas. Response: The response given to this is that a ruling established by qiyas is in fact established by the speech of Allah, but it is hidden; qiyas only manifests it. So it is established that in qiyas there is also the speech of Allah, and the ruling is established by it; qiyas is only a revealer, not an establisher. And it is not hidden from you that the questioner is objecting to the author: to specify the third objection with what is established by qiyas is not correct, because this problem applies not only to what is established by qiyas but also to what is established by sunnah and what is established by ijma. Because a ruling established by sunnah is not established by the speech of Allah but by the speech of the Messenger, and likewise a ruling established by ijma is not established by the speech of Allah. Hence, specification with what is established by qiyas is not correct. Response: The response to this is that here too, in reality, the ruling is established by the speech of Allah; sunnah and ijma are only revealers and identifiers of this ruling and the speech, and they indicate that here too the speech exists. And the meaning of sunnah and ijma being evidences for rulings is that they are revealers and identifiers of rulings. Fourth: It is not inclusive—explanation of the fourth objection: the definition of hukm is not comprehensive; it does not apply to 'Believe' (amanu) and 'So take heed' (fa'tabiru) because the definition mentions the actions of the responsible persons (af'al al-mukallafin), and by actions, apparently outward actions (af'al al-jawarih) are meant, i.e., those performed by the outer limbs. Hence, speech related to actions of the heart (af'al al-qalb) would be excluded from the definition, such as the obligation of faith (wujub al-iman) meaning belief, and the obligation of consideration (wujub al-i'tibar) meaning qiyas. These are actions of the heart, not actions of the limbs, so the definition of hukm would not apply to them and would not be comprehensive. Fifth: Since he took—explanation of the fifth objection: that in the definition of fiqh, the restriction 'al-`amaliyyah' (practical) is redundant because when the definition of hukm includes the restriction 'actions of the responsible persons' and by actions, outward actions are meant, then actions of the responsible persons become specific to outward acts (al-`amalat), and theoretical rulings (ahkam nazariyyah) are excluded from it. Hence, after that, the restriction 'al-`amaliyyah' in the definition of fiqh is superfluous and unnecessary, because its purpose was also to exclude theoretical matters, and they are already excluded by 'actions of the responsible persons'. And he answered both—the commentator Taftazani transmits the same answer given by the author: that 'actions of the responsible persons' is general, whether they are actions of the limbs or actions of the heart, so the definition of hukm includes both and becomes comprehensive. Thus the fourth objection falls. And in the definition of fiqh, the restriction 'al-`amaliyyah' is meant only for actions of the limbs and outward acts; thereby theoretical rulings are excluded, such as the obligation of faith, the obligation of consideration, etc. Hence, mentioning 'al-`amaliyyah' is not redundant, and thus the fifth objection is also removed. And someone may say: 'When the ruling is made...' This is an objection against the author that even if 'actions of the responsible persons' is interpreted generally, the restriction 'al-`amaliyyah' still entails redundancy. This restriction is redundant in any case because by it two things were intended to be excluded: (1) the obligation of faith and the like, and (2) the fact that ijma is a proof. And both of these are already excluded before imposing the restriction 'al-`amaliyyah'. The obligation of faith etc. are excluded by the restriction 'al-shar`iyyah' because it was previously mentioned that if in the definition of fiqh, 'hukm' means hukm in the technical sense, then 'al-shar`iyyah' means 'what depends on the law'. When 'al-shar`iyyah' means 'what depends on the law', then the obligation of faith and the like are excluded from it because they are not dependent on the law. (2) Second, by this, theoretical rulings such as that ijma is a proof, etc., are intended to be excluded, and they are excluded by the restriction in the definition of hukm that is 'requirement or option' (iqtida' or takhyir), because in the fact that ijma is a proof, etc.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) on Taftazani's commentary on Al-Tawdih. The author first corrects Taftazani's error regarding the nature of legal soundness and corruption, arguing they are legal, not rational. He then addresses four objections to the definitions of hukm and fiqh. The first objection (misnumbered as fourth in the text) claims that the mandub status of a child's prayer relates to the guardian, not the child; the author refutes this by citing the hadith 'Command them to pray at seven' and examples of reward for children's acts. The second objection (third) argues that the definition of hukm excludes rulings from qiyas, sunnah, and ijma since they are not directly from Allah's speech; the response is that these sources merely reveal the divine ruling, which is already present in Allah's speech. The third objection (fourth) claims the definition excludes actions of the heart (e.g., faith, reasoning); the response is that 'actions of the responsible persons' includes heart actions. The fourth objection (fifth) claims the term 'amaliyyah in the definition of fiqh is redundant; the response is that 'amaliyyah specifically limits fiqh to outward actions, while theoretical matters like the obligation of faith are kept distinct. An additional objection is raised that even with generalization, redundancy remains, and the author begins to address it by showing how certain theoretical matters are excluded by other parts of the definition. The passage demonstrates the Hanafi usul method of defending definitions against objections using textual proofs and rational analysis.

Key Terms
افعال جوارح (afʿāl al-jawāriḥ): Actions of the limbs; outward physical acts. Distinguished from actions of the heart.
افعال قلب (afʿāl al-qalb): Actions of the heart; inner acts such as belief, intention, reasoning.
احکام نظریہ (aḥkām naẓariyyah): Theoretical rulings; e.g., that ijma is a proof. These are not actions, so excluded from fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the term 'amaliyyah not redundant in the definition of fiqh?
A) Because it includes heart actions.
B) Because it restricts fiqh to limb actions, excluding theoretical matters.
C) Because it emphasizes practical implementation.
D) Because it removes the condition 'shar'iyyah'.

Key Takeaway
Actions of the heart are included in the definition of hukm, but fiqh is restricted to practical limb actions via 'amaliyyah.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Clarifying the Definitions of Hukm and Fiqh: Replying to Two Objections
Responding to two objections about definitions of hukm and fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi definition of hukm includes heart actions and why the term 'al-ʿamaliyya' in the definition of fiqh is not redundant.

Core Idea
Hukm covers heart and limb actions; 'al-ʿamaliyya' excludes theoretical rulings, not redundantly.

Definitions in usul al-fiqh are carefully crafted to include everything needed and exclude what should be excluded. Two objections challenge the definitions of hukm and fiqh, claiming either incompleteness or redundancy. Understanding how scholars like Taftazani respond sharpens your ability to evaluate and refine legal definitions—a key skill in usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fourth objection
Find the claim that the definition of hukm does not cover acts of the heart such as faith (iman) and reflection (iʿtibar). Understand why the objector thinks 'actions of the mukallaf' means only outward limb actions.
Look for the example 'آمنوا' and 'فاعتبروا'.
2
Identify the fifth objection
Locate the claim that the word 'al-ʿamaliyya' in the definition of fiqh is redundant because the definition of hukm already limits to actions of the mukallaf, which are understood as practical.
The objection rests on assuming 'actions' means only limb actions.
3
Analyze Taftazani's response to the fourth objection
Note that he argues 'actions of the mukallaf' is general and includes both limb and heart actions. This is supported by the fact that divine address commands both outward acts (e.g., prayer) and inward acts (e.g., belief).
Thus the fourth objection fails because the definition is comprehensive.
4
Analyze Taftazani's response to the fifth objection
He clarifies that 'al-ʿamaliyya' specifically excludes theoretical rulings such as the authority of consensus (kawn al-ijmaʿ ḥujja). These are not actions of the mukallaf at all, so they were not excluded by the definition of hukm. So the qualification is needed.
He further distinguishes rulings that involve a demand (iqtidaʾ) from those that do not, showing the refinement.

The fourth (objection) is that it is not comprehensive: the definition of hukm does not apply to 'believe' and 'take heed' because it mentions actions of the mukallaf, and actions apparently mean outward limb actions, so addresses concerning heart actions would be excluded, like the obligation of faith as affirmation and obligation of reflection as analogy. These are heart actions, not limb actions, so the definition of hukm does not apply and is not comprehensive. The fifth: the term 'al-'amaliyya' in the definition of fiqh is redundant because when actions of the mukallaf are specified and meant as limb actions, they become specific to practical actions, and theoretical rulings are thereby excluded; so mentioning 'al-'amaliyya' is superfluous. The commentator Taftazani responds that actions of the mukallaf are general including limb and heart actions, so the fourth objection is invalid. And by 'al-'amaliyya' in fiqh only limb actions and practices are meant, excluding theoretical rulings, so the fifth objection is removed. If someone objects that even with this division the qualification is redundant because it excludes two things already excluded: obligation of faith etc. by the term 'shar'iyya', and theoretical rulings like authority of consensus by the term 'bi al-iqtida' or 'tahayyur', Taftazani replies that rulings like 'authority of consensus' are not included in 'bi al-iqtida' because they do not involve demand, but rulings like 'permissibility of consensus' and 'obligation of analogy' do involve demand but are still not practical, so they are excluded by 'al-'amaliyya', making the qualification useful. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage addresses two logical objections to the definitions of hukm (legal ruling) and fiqh (jurisprudence). The fourth objection states that the definition of hukm as 'address concerning actions of the mukallaf' fails to include acts of the heart like faith (iman) and reflection (i'tibar), which are commanded by revelation. The fifth objection argues that in the definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of practical shar'i rulings derived from detailed evidence,' the word 'practical' (al-'amaliyya) is redundant because the definition of hukm already limits to actions of the mukallaf, which are understood as outward actions, thereby excluding theoretical rulings. Taftazani responds by clarifying that 'actions of the mukallaf' in the definition of hukm includes both outward and inward actions, so the fourth objection falls. For the fifth, he explains that 'al-'amaliyya' specifically excludes theoretical issues like the authority of consensus (kawn al-ijma' hujja) which are not practical actions; this is not redundant because those issues were not excluded by 'actions of the mukallaf' since they are not actions at all. He further refines by distinguishing rulings that involve a demand (iqtida') from those that do not. This illustrates the precision required in usul definitions.

Key Terms
حكم (hukm): A legal ruling addressed to the actions of the mukallaf, including both outward limb actions and inward heart actions.
العملیة (al-ʿamaliyya): Relating to practical actions (of the limbs), excluding theoretical rulings in the definition of fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the response to the fifth objection, why is the term 'al-ʿamaliyya' not redundant in the definition of fiqh?
A) Because it includes heart actions like faith.
B) Because it excludes theoretical rulings that are not excluded by the definition of hukm.
C) Because it emphasizes that fiqh only concerns outward actions.
D) Because it is required for the definition to be comprehensive.

Key Takeaway
The term 'afʿāl al-mukallafīn' in the definition of hukm includes both heart and limb actions, and 'al-ʿamaliyya' in fiqh is necessary to exclude theoretical rulings that are not actions at all.

What Makes a Ruling Shar'i? The Hanafi Definition and the Role of Analogy
Defining shar'iyya and beginning critique of Ash'ari ethical theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of shar'iyya and explain why analogical rulings are included within it.

Core Idea
A ruling is shar'i if its knowledge depends on revelation, even indirectly via analogy.

We have learned what a hukm (legal ruling) is. Now we must clarify what makes a ruling 'shar'i' (legal/religious). The Hanafi position is broad: a ruling is shar'i if its knowledge would be impossible without the address of the Lawgiver (khitab al-shari'). This includes not only rulings explicitly stated in the Quran or Sunnah but also those derived by analogy (qiyas), because the analogical ruling depends on the revelation that established the original case. This definition has profound implications for how we understand the scope of fiqh and the role of reason.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the core definition
Read the author's definition: 'al-shar'iyya ma la tudrak lawla khitab al-shari'' — 'shar'iyya is that which cannot be known without the address of the Lawgiver.' Identify the key condition: without revelation, the ruling's knowledge is impossible.
Note the negation 'lawla' — it indicates that the revelation is the indispensable condition for knowledge.
2
Distinguish direct vs. indirect dependence
The commentator explains two cases: (1) the ruling is directly stated in revelation; (2) the ruling is not directly stated, but it depends on a revelation that occurs in another place (e.g., the original case in qiyas). Recognize that both are included because the knowledge of the ruling ultimately traces back to revelation.
This is the Hanafi justification for including qiyas in the scope of shar'i rulings.
3
Connect to the broader debate on ethics
The passage then begins to critique the Ash'ari position that good and evil are learned only from revelation. The Hanafi view that some actions have rational goodness/evil is introduced. Understand that this debate hinges on whether reason alone can ground ethical value before revelation.
This step sets the stage for future lessons; for now, note the two schools of thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متن (توضيح) والشرعية مالا تدرك لولا خطاب الشارع الخ حکم کی تفصیل و تشریح کرنے کے بعد مصنف الشرعیہ کی تعریف اور تشریح کر رہے ہیں الشرعیہ کی تعریف یہ کی مالا تدرک لولا خطاب الشارع شرعی وہ حکم ہے کہ اگر شارع کا خطاب نہ ہوتا تو اس کا علم حاصل نہ ہوتا بعنوان دیگر شرعی وہ ہے ما توقف على الشرع خواہ وہ خطاب شارع بعینہ اسی حکم میں وارد ہوا ہو یا بعینہ اسی میں وارد نہ ہوا ہو لیکن خطاب شارع ایسی جگہ میں وارد ہوا ہو جس کی طرف یہ حکم محتاج ہو وہ جگہ محتاج الیہ ہواگر اس جگہ میں خطاب شارع نہ ہوتا تو اس حکم محتاج کا بھی اور اک نہ ہو سکتا جیسے مسائل قیاسیہ کیونکہ مسائل قیاسیہ میں خطاب بعینہ اس حکم قیاسی میں وارد نہیں ہوتا بلکہ مقیس علیہ میں وارد ہوتا ہے اور اس کی طرف حکم مقیس محتاج ہوتا ہے اگر مقیس علیہ میں خطاب شارع وارد نہ ہوتا تو مقیس والے حکم کا ادراک و علم حاصل نہ ہوتا۔ فیدخل في حد الفقه حسن كل شئى وقبحه یہاں سے مصنف اشاعرہ پر اعتراض کرنا چاہتے ہیں جسکی تفصیل اس طرح ہے کہ احناف کے اور اکثر معتزلہ کے نزدیک افعال کی دو قسمیں ہیں (۱) بعض افعال ایسے ہیں کہ ان کا حسن اور فتح عقلی ہیں یعنی ان کا حسن اور فتح مدرک
Text (Tawdih): 'And al-shar'iyya is that which cannot be known without the address of the Lawgiver.' After detailing and explaining hukm, the author defines and explains shar'iyya: the definition of shar'iyya is 'that which cannot be known without the address of the Lawgiver'; a shar'i ruling is one whose knowledge would not be obtained if there were no address of the Lawgiver. In another sense, shar'i is that which depends on the Law, whether the address occurs specifically in that ruling or not, but the address occurs in a place to which this ruling is in need; that place is the needed thing. If there were no address of the Lawgiver in that place, the knowledge of the dependent ruling could not be obtained, such as analogical issues because in analogical issues the address does not occur specifically in that analogical ruling, but occurs in the original case (maqis alayh), and the analogical ruling depends on it. If the address of the Lawgiver did not occur in the original case, the knowledge and understanding of the analogical ruling would not be obtained. 'So enters into the definition of fiqh the goodness and evil of everything' – from here the author intends to object to the Ash'aris. The detail is that according to the Hanafis and most Mu'tazila, actions are of two types: (1) some actions whose goodness and evil are rational, i.e., their goodness and evil are perceived intellectually.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage defines 'shar'iyya' (legal/religious) for rulings. A ruling is shar'i if its knowledge depends on the address of the Lawgiver (khitab al-shari'). This includes not only rulings directly stated in revelation but also those derived by analogy (qiyas), because the analogical ruling depends on the revealed address in the original case. The author then begins a critique of the Ash'ari school, which holds that good and evil are known only through revelation, whereas Hanafis and Mu'tazila affirm rational ethical valuation. This is a foundational usul difference: the Hanafi position allows reason to play a role in determining the goodness or badness of actions prior to revelation, which affects the derivation of rulings. The passage sets up the debate, noting that for Hanafis, some actions have intrinsic rational goodness or evil, and this is relevant to fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
شرعية (shar'iyya): Property of a ruling that cannot be known without the Lawgiver's address; depends on revelation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition in this passage, why is a ruling derived by qiyas considered shar'i?
A) Because the ruling is rationally self-evident.
B) Because it depends on the revealed text that serves as the basis for the analogy.
C) Because the Prophet (ﷺ) explicitly mentioned all analogical cases.
D) Because qiyas is a separate source of law independent of revelation.

Key Takeaway
A shar'i ruling is one whose knowledge is impossible without revelation, which includes both directly stated rulings and those derived via qiyas due to their dependence on a revealed original case.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Definition of a Sharʿī Ruling
Defining sharʿī ruling and its inclusion of qiyās-derived rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of a sharʿī ruling and explain why qiyāsī rulings are included.

Core Idea
A sharʿī ruling depends on divine speech, directly or through qiyās.

In this lesson, we explore the foundational definition of a sharʿī (sharʿī) ruling in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The passage clarifies that a ruling is sharʿī if it cannot be known without the speech of the Lawgiver, and crucially, this includes rulings derived through qiyās (analogy). Understanding this definition is key to recognizing the scope of fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The passage defines 'al-sharʿiyya' — the sharʿī rulings. Note that the term refers to rulings that are considered part of the Sharīʿah.
The definiendum is the category of rulings under discussion.
2
Parse the definition
The definition given is 'mā lā tudriku laulā khiṭāb al-shāriʿ' — what cannot be perceived without the speech of the Lawgiver. Break down the key components: 'mā lā tudriku' (what is not known), 'laulā' (if not for), 'khiṭāb al-shāriʿ' (the speech of the Lawgiver).
The definition establishes a conditional dependence.
3
Understand the condition
The phrase 'laulā khiṭāb al-shāriʿ' indicates that the ruling's knowability is contingent on divine speech. If the Lawgiver had not spoken, the ruling would not be known.
This excludes rulings that are rationally self-evident or known by pure reason.
4
Apply to qiyās
The passage explicitly extends the definition to qiyāsī rulings. Even though the Lawgiver's speech does not directly address the derived case, it addresses the original case (maqīs ʿalayh). The derived case depends on that original text. Without it, the analogy would not be possible.
Thus qiyāsī rulings are sharʿī because they ultimately rely on divine speech.
5
Distinguish from rational rulings
Contrast this with rulings that would be known independently of revelation, such as the goodness of justice or the evil of lying. Those are not considered sharʿī in the technical sense used here.
This distinction is essential for defining the scope of fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متن (توضيح)
والشرعية مالا تدرك لولا خطاب الشارع الخ حکم کی تفصیل و تشریح کرنے کے بعد مصنف الشرعیہ کی تعریف اور تشریح کر رہے ہیں الشرعیہ کی تعریف یہ کی مالا تدرک لولا خطاب الشارع شرعی وہ حکم ہے کہ اگر شارع کا خطاب نہ ہوتا تو اس کا علم حاصل نہ ہوتا بعنوان دیگر شرعی وہ ہے ما توقف على الشرع خواہ وہ خطاب شارع بعینہ اسی حکم . میں وارد ہوا ہو یا بعینہ اسی میں وارد نہ ہوا ہو لیکن خطاب شارع ایسی جگہ میں وارد ہوا ہو جس کی طرف یہ حکم محتاج ہو وہ جگہ محتاج الیہ ہواگر اس جگہ میں خطاب شارع نہ ہوتا تو اس حکم محتاج کا بھی اور اک نہ ہو سکتا جیسے مسائل قیاسیہ کیونکہ مسائل قیاسیہ میں خطاب بعینہ اس حکم قیاسی میں وارد نہیں ہوتا بلکہ مقیس علیہ میں وارد ہوتا ہے اور اس کی طرف حکم مقیس محتاج ہوتا ہے اگر مقیس علیہ میں خطاب شارع وارد نہ ہوتا تو مقیس والے حکم کا ادراک و علم حاصل نہ ہوتا۔
Matn (al-Tawḍīḥ): 'And al-sharʿiyya (the sharʿī rulings) are what cannot be known without the speech of the Lawgiver, etc.' After detailing and explaining the ḥukm, the author is defining and explaining al-sharʿiyya. The definition of al-sharʿiyya is: mā lā tudriku laulā khiṭāb al-shāriʿ: a sharʿī ruling is that whose knowledge would not be obtained if the Lawgiver's speech were absent. In another sense, sharʿī is that which depends on the sharʿ, whether the Lawgiver's speech has occurred in that very ruling, or it has not occurred in it directly, but the Lawgiver's speech has occurred in a place to which this ruling is in need; that place is the needful place; if the Lawgiver's speech were absent in that place, then the knowledge of this needful ruling would also not be possible, like qiyāsī issues. Because in qiyāsī issues, the speech does not occur in the very ruling of the qiyās, but it occurs in the maqīs ʿalayh (the case to which analogy is made), and the maqīs (the case being analogized) is in need of that. If the Lawgiver's speech were not present in the maqīs ʿalayh, then the perception and knowledge of the maqīs ruling would not be obtained.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharḥ) on the matn of al-Tawḍīḥ. It clarifies the definition of a sharʿī (sharʿī) ruling. The definition given is: a ruling that cannot be known without the speech of the Lawgiver. This includes both direct rulings from the text and rulings derived through qiyās (analogy). The commentator explains that for qiyāsī rulings, the Lawgiver's speech does not directly address that ruling, but it addresses the original case (maqīs ʿalayh), and the derived case depends on that. Without the original text, the analogy would not be possible. This definition ensures that qiyāsī rulings are considered sharʿī, as they ultimately depend on divine speech.

Key Terms
الشرعیة (al-sharʿiyya): The sharʿī rulings; rulings that cannot be known without the speech of the Lawgiver

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are qiyāsī rulings considered sharʿī?
A) Because they are directly mentioned in the Qur'an.
B) Because they depend on the Lawgiver's speech indirectly through the original case.
C) Because they are rationally known.
D) Because they are agreed upon by all mujtahids.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a ruling is sharʿī if it cannot be known without divine speech, whether directly or through qiyās.

The Ash'ari Objection and the Definition of Fiqh
Objection to Ash'ari view that all good/bad is sharia-based, affecting the definition of fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Ash'ari doctrine regarding al-ḥusn wa al-qubḥ and evaluate its impact on the definition of fiqh according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
If all goodness is known only from Sharia, then ethics would be part of fiqh — but fiqh is not ethics.

This passage tackles a major theological-legal dispute between the Hanafis and Ash'aris. At stake is the very definition of fiqh: is it knowledge of all shar'i rulings, or only those not known by reason? The Ash'ari position threatens to make the definition overinclusive. The Hanafi response preserves the preclusive nature of the definition by distinguishing rational from shar'i moral values.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two schools' positions on moral knowledge
State clearly: Hanafis (and Mu'tazila) hold that some acts (e.g., generosity) are good rationally, independent of Sharia. Ash'aris hold that all moral values are known only through Sharia.
This is a foundational epistemological difference.
2
Understand how each school defines fiqh
Hanafis define fiqh as knowledge of shar'i rulings only. Ash'aris would have to define it more broadly, including all knowledge of good and bad since all are shar'i.
3
Follow the Hanafi objection
If all good/bad are shar'i, then knowledge of the virtue of generosity would be a shar'i ruling and thus part of fiqh. But this is not the case — ethics is separate. Hence the definition would not be preclusive (māniʿ).
Preclusive means it excludes non-fiqh knowledge.
4
Recognize the practical consequence
The dispute determines the scope of fiqh: whether it includes ethical knowledge (Ash'ari) or is limited to rulings dependent on Sharia (Hanafi).
The thamarah al-khilaf is a difference in the definition itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيدخل في حد الفقه حسن كل شئى وقبحه یہاں سے مصنف اشاعرہ پر اعتراض کرنا چاہتے ہیں جسکی تفصیل اس طرح ہے کہ احناف کے اور اکثر معتزلہ کے نزدیک افعال کی دو قسمیں ہیں (۱) بعض افعال ایسے ہیں کہ ان کا حسن اور فتح عقلی ہیں یعنی ان کا حسن اور فتح مدرک با عقل ہوتا ہے شریعت پر موقوف نہیں ہوتا (۲) بعض افعال ایسے ہیں کہ ان کا حسن اور صبح مدرک بالشرع ہوتا ہے شریعت پر موقوف ہوتا ہے اول کی مثال حسن تواضع، حج تمبر، حسن جود اور صبح بخل یہ شریعت پر موقوف نہیں بلکہ ان کا ادراک عقل کے ساتھ ہوتا ہے ثانی کی مثال رمضان المبارک کی تیسویں کے صوم کا حسن اور یکم شوال ( عید الفطر) کے روزہ کا فتح ان کا ادراک صرف شریعت سے ہو سکتا ہے عقل سے نہیں امام ابوالحسن اشعری واتباعہ کے نزدیک ہرشئی کا حسن اور فتح شرعی ہے شریعت پر موقوف ہے کسی چیز کا حسن اور فتح عقل کے ساتھ ادراک نہیں کیا جا سکتا اب اشاعرہ پر اشکال یہ ہوتا ہے کہ جب آپ کے نزدیک ہر چیز کا حسن اور فتح شرعی ہے تو فقہ کی تعریف مانع نہیں رہے گی ہر چیز کے حسن اور فتح کا جو علم دلیل سے حاصل ہو گا وہ فقہ میں داخل ہو جائے گا مثلا حسن تواضع حسن جود، فتح تکبر فتح بحل یہ سب فقہ اصطلاحی میں داخل ہو جائیں گے حالانکہ یہ فقہ اصطلاحی میں داخل نہیں تو تعریف فقہ مانع نہیں رہے گی جب کہ ہمارے نزدیک تعریف فقہ مانع ہوگی کیونکہ حسن جود وغیرہ جو علم الاخلاق میں سے ہیں وہ فقہ کی تعریف میں داخل نہیں ہونگے کیونکہ ان کا حسن و جج شرعی نہیں ہے بلکہ عقلی ہے
This enters into the definition of fiqh the good (ḥusn) and bad (qubḥ) of everything. From here the author intends to object to the Ashʿarīs. The explanation is that, according to the Ḥanafīs and most Muʿtazila, acts are of two types: (1) Some acts have rational good and bad, i.e., their good and bad are perceived by the intellect, not dependent on the Sharīʿah. (2) Some acts have sharʿī good and bad, dependent on the Sharīʿah. Example of the first: the virtue of humility, the virtue of generosity, and the evil of stinginess – these are not dependent on the Sharīʿah but are perceived by the intellect. Example of the second: the goodness of fasting on the 30th of Ramaḍān and the badness of fasting on the 1st of Shawwāl (ʿĪd al-Fiṭr); their perception is only through the Sharīʿah, not the intellect. According to Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī and his followers, the good and bad of everything are sharʿī, dependent on the Sharīʿah; nothing is perceived by the intellect. Now the objection to the Ashʿarīs is: Since according to you the good and bad of everything are sharʿī, the definition of fiqh will not be preclusive. Every knowledge of good and bad obtained through evidence will enter into fiqh. For example, the virtue of humility, virtue of generosity, evil of arrogance, evil of stinginess – all these would enter into technical fiqh, whereas they are not part of technical fiqh. Hence the definition of fiqh would not be preclusive. Whereas according to us, the definition of fiqh is preclusive because the goodness of generosity etc., which are part of ethics (ʿilm al-akhlāq), are not included in the definition of fiqh, because their good and bad are not sharʿī but rational.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of fiqh. It presents an objection to the Ashʿarī school of theology. According to the Ashʿarīs, all moral values (ḥusn and qubḥ) are determined by the Sharīʿah; reason cannot independently know good or bad. The Ḥanafīs, on the other hand, hold that some acts are intrinsically good or bad and can be known by reason (e.g., justice, lying). The objection is that if the Ashʿarīs are correct, then the definition of fiqh (knowledge of sharʿī rulings) would include all knowledge of good and bad – such as the virtue of generosity – which are actually part of ethics, not fiqh. Thus the definition would not be preclusive (māniʿ). The Ḥanafī response is that their own definition is preclusive because rationally known good/bad are not sharʿī. This section underscores the importance of the distinction between rational and sharʿī values in defining fiqh.

Key Terms
الحسن والفتح (al-ḥusnu wa al-qubḥ): Good and bad; moral values of acts

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafī author claim that the Ashʿarī position would make the definition of fiqh non-preclusive?
A) Because the Ashʿarīs believe that fiqh includes all knowledge.
B) Because if all good/bad are sharʿī, then ethical virtues like generosity would be considered fiqh.
C) Because the Ashʿarīs exclude qiyās from fiqh.
D) Because the Hanafīs define fiqh narrower.

Key Takeaway
The definition of fiqh depends on the epistemology of moral values: Hanafis separate rational from sharʿī, Ash'aris unify them.
